Chinn/The San Francisco Chronicle via Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Over the past week, the US reported an average of nearly 50,000 new COVID-19 infections each day, according to CDC data.</li>\n<li>The highest number of new cases was reported Friday - with more than 81,000 new cases reported.</li>\n<li>That puts the US in a situation not seen since the spring as US officials reportedly reconsider mask guidance.</li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>Over the past week, the US has reported an average of nearly 50,000 COVID-19 cases each day, according to <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2021/us/covid-cases.html\">data analyzed by The New York Times</a>, putting the number of average daily infections at the highest number since the beginning May. </p><p>There were more than 81,000 new cases of the disease reported in the US on Friday, according to the NYT data, which is aggregated from the US Centers for Disease Control and Prevention and from state governments. That&#39;s the highest number of new cases diagnosed in the US in a single day since February 13 when more than 84,000 cases were diagnosed, according to the data.</p><p>The rise in new infections come amid the spread of the <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/delta-covid-variant-cases-threatening-economy-markets-reopening-lockdown-02021-7\">more contagious delta variant of the disease</a>. CDC Director Rochelle Walensky previously <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/cdc-director-says-covid-19-hospitalizations-are-mostly-unvaccinated-2021-7\">called the uptick in cases a &#34;pandemic of the unvaccinated.&#34;</a></p><p>While <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/covid-after-vaccine-symptoms-to-expect-breakthrough-cases-2021-7\">breakthrough infections</a> - cases where vaccinated people test positive for the disease - can occur, the vaccines are effective in <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/us-coronavirus-deaths-nearly-all-among-unvaccinated-cdc-head-2021-7\">preventing serious illness, hospitalizations, and death</a>, according to the CDC. </p><p>The rise in cases has reopened the debate on the wearing of face masks in public settings. The CDC in May updated its guidance on masks, saying face coverings weren&#39;t necessary for fully vaccinated people in most settings. It still advises that unvaccinated people wear them, especially when indoors.</p><p>States and businesses in the US followed suit, ditching the mask requirement for vaccinated individuals.</p><p>But some areas are now taking a more cautious approach, once again encouraging all people, regardless of vaccination status, to mask up in public. Officials in Los Angeles County in California reinstated their mask mandate earlier in July, <a href=\"https://www.insider.com/los-angeles-county-reinstates-indoor-mask-mandate-covid-19-surge-2021-7\">calling the effort to stop rising case numbers an &#34;all-hands-on-deck moment.&#34; </a></p><p>Officials in St. Louis plan to reinstate their mask mandate on Monday, <a href=\"https://fox2now.com/news/mask-mandate-returns-to-st-louis-city-and-county-monday/\">according to Fox 2</a>.</p><p>The American Academy of Pediatrics earlier this week recommended that <a href=\"https://services.aap.org/en/news-room/news-releases/aap/2021/american-academy-of-pediatrics-updates-recommendations-for-opening-schools-in-fall-2021/\">all students and staff at schools wear a mask even if they&#39;re vaccinated against the disease</a> when they return to classrooms. President Joe Biden on Wednesday said the CDC would likely <a href=\"https://www.reuters.com/world/us/biden-says-cdc-will-advise-unvaccinated-kids-mask-up-school-this-fall-2021-07-22/\">recommend that unvaccinated children wear masks in the fall</a>.</p><p>The <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/biden-administration-considers-stricter-mask-guidance-as-delta-variant-spreads-2021-7\">Biden administration and the CDC are considering reintroducing stricter mask guidance</a>, the Washington Post reported this week, in order to protect unvaccinated people, who make up the majority of hospitalizations and deaths from the disease.</p><p>According <a href=\"https://covid.cdc.gov/covid-data-tracker/#vaccinations\">to CDC data</a>, about 49% of the US population is fully vaccinated against COVID-19. Slightly more - about 57% - of the US population is partially vaccinated against the disease. Nearly 80% of the US population aged 65 or older is fully vaccinated, which is the highest of any age demographic, per the CDC.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a +0000","feedId":22,"bgimg":"https://i.insider.com/60fbeca917cf960019ee00a7?format=jpeg","linkMd5":"b0f3068df68d0a0b71465e2ee9a0854f","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn52@2020_1/2021/07/25/10-54-21-701_f7925139bd44a254.webp","destWidth":2698,"destHeight":2024,"sourceBytes":106012,"destBytes":106012,"author":"kshalvey@insider.com Shalvey)","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i.insider.com/60fbec9828e2cf0018905951?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn60@2020_3/2021/07/25/10-54-24-937_29f0189309dd9df7.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn39@2020_3/2021/07/25/10-54-25-234_cc3ef43fc8ff5801.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=x6mrDFE9O2s:xJjhf4HEITE:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn72@2020_4/2021/07/25/10-54-24-351_4cb447e76e47cb68.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=x6mrDFE9O2s:xJjhf4HEITE:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn48@2020_6/2021/07/25/10-54-25-301_c4ca02b2909dc113.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn95@2020_6/2021/07/25/10-54-23-641_740defb435ea442b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=x6mrDFE9O2s:xJjhf4HEITE:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn83@2020_2/2021/07/25/10-54-24-419_12c2ee2cfd5c440c.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/x6mrDFE9O2s":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn40@2020_5/2021/07/25/10-54-25-432_2adefe3d0f21f303.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1627210446777},{"createdTime":"2021-07-25 18:54:06","updatedTime":"2021-07-25 18:54:06","title":"NASA's Mars helicopter nailed its 10th flight - double what engineers had hoped Ingenuity would do","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/nasa-mars-ingenuity-helicopter-10th-flight-2021-7","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/606b3ae0a7d803001963ebe2?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Mars ingenuity helicopter nasa perseverance rover\" height=\"960\" width=\"1280\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>The Ingenuity helicopter on Mars, sitting where the Perseverance rover dropped it, on April 5, 2021.\n<p class=\"copyright\"><a href=\"https://mars.nasa.gov/mars2020/multimedia/raw-images/NLF_0044_0670852159_569ECM_N0031416NCAM03044_01_295J\" target=\"_blank\">NASA/JPL-Caltech</a></p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>NASA&#39;s Ingenuity helicopter completed its 10th flight on Mars Saturday.</li>\n<li>That&#39;s twice the number of flights that NASA originally planned for the little drone. </li>\n<li>Engineers thought Ingenuity would crash much earlier, but it&#39;s now on an extended mission.</li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>The &#34;little helicopter that could&#34; has done it again.</p><p>NASA&#39;s Ingenuity helicopter on Mars, a tissue-box-sized rotorcraft that landed with the Perseverance rover in February, completed its 10th flight over the red planet on Saturday.</p><p>Each Ingenuity flight has been more daring than the last. So Saturday&#39;s flight was likely the helicopter&#39;s riskiest yet: If everything went according to plan, Ingenuity climbed 40 feet in the air, then headed south-by-southwest toward a collection of rock features called &#34;Raised Ridges,&#34; before looping back around to a landing zone about 310 feet west of its initial takeoff spot. </p><p>Before Saturday, Ingenuity had already flown nearly one mile in total, so its 10th flight helped it <a href=\"https://twitter.com/nasajpl/status/1419152522757894147?s=21\">hit that threshold</a>.</p><p>The flight should have lasted about 2 minutes, 45 seconds. During that time, Ingenuity is expected to have visited 10 distinct waypoints, snapping photos along the way.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/60fb555728e2cf0018905738?format=jpeg\" alt=\"ingenuity flight 10 path\" height=\"784\" width=\"1224\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>An annotated image of Mars’ Jezero Crater depicts the ground track and waypoints for Ingenuity’s 10th flight.\n<p class=\"copyright\">NASA/JPL-Caltech/University of Arizona</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>Flight 10 is a significant milestone, since Ingenuity has now flown twice as many times as NASA engineers originally planned. NASA expected Ingenuity to crash on its fourth or fifth flight as it tested the limits of its speed and stamina.</p><p>But Ingenuity has continued to exceed expectations. Even when <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/nasa-ingenuity-mars-helicopter-survived-mid-flight-error-2021-5\">a glitch</a> led the helicopter to wobble mid-air in May during its sixth flight, it still managed to touch down safely.</p><p>The drone started out as a technology demonstration, but NASA gave Ingenuity <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/nasa-ingenuity-mars-helicopter-to-start-scouting-2021-4\" data-analytics-module=\"body_link\" data-analytics-post-depth=\"20\" data-uri=\"9a21f1b74edb60f5f0d2ce291f4d95f1\">a secondary mission</a> in late May after its fourth flight. Since then, Ingenuity has started scouting new Martian terrain and testing operations that NASA might want to conduct with future space helicopters. In its recent flights, Ingenuity has explored unsurveyed areas of <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/nasa-mars-rover-landing-jezero-crater-search-for-alien-life-2021-2\">Mars&#39; Jezero Crater</a> - a 28-mile-wide impact basin that was filled with water about 3.5 billion years ago - landing in new spots each time.</p><p>The uneven landscape is a challenge for Ingenuity, since rocky or rippled land can distort its field of view, causing it to veer in the wrong direction. Ingenuity&#39;s ninth flight earlier this month was a &#34;nail-biter,&#34; <a href=\"https://mars.nasa.gov/technology/helicopter/status/314/flight-9-was-a-nail-biter-but-ingenuity-came-through-with-flying-colors/\">NASA scientists said</a>, since the helicopter had to cross over particularly treacherous terrain.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/608ad48735c46f0018c0b3ec?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Ingenuity helicopter mars first flight Taking Off and Landing\" height=\"720\" width=\"1280\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>The Perseverance rover captured footage of Ingenuity taking off and landing for its first flight on April 19, 2021.\n<p class=\"copyright\"><a href=\"https://mars.nasa.gov/resources/25838/mastcam-z-video-of-ingenuity-taking-off-and-landing/\" target=\"_blank\">NASA/JPL-Caltech/ASU/MSSS</a></p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><h2>Ingenuity is still proving itself useful on Mars, but its future is uncertain</h2><p>In Ingenuity&#39;s first four flights, the rotorcraft landed in the same spot it lifted off. Its fifth flight led it to touch down in a new airfield that it had previously flown over, photographed, and mapped. But these recent flights have sent Ingenuity traveling south over uncharted territory.</p><p>NASA engineers haven&#39;t said when Ingenuity&#39;s mission will end, but the helicopter could keep flying as long as it stays alive and doesn&#39;t interfere with the science work of the Perseverance rover.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/60bfffc5fde7960018aacd9b?format=jpeg\" alt=\"ingenuity flight seven shadow\" height=\"480\" width=\"640\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Ingenuity captured a photo of its shadow during its seventh flight on Mars.\n<p class=\"copyright\">NASA/JPL-Caltech</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>Perseverance is combing Jezero Crater in search of <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/nasa-mars-rover-landing-jezero-crater-search-for-alien-life-2021-2\" data-analytics-module=\"body_link\" data-analytics-post-depth=\"100\" data-uri=\"e4cea5940cb6a3bb3c6b01eadc6ba292\">potential fossils of ancient alien microbes</a>. Ingenuity&#39;s new operations can assist with that mission: The helicopter can scout and map terrain, spot promising areas for study from the air, and fly to spots the rover can&#39;t reach.</p><p>NASA scientists are particularly curious about &#34;Raised Ridges,&#34; since water may have once flowed there. During its ninth flight, Ingenuity also snapped color images of intriguing rock outcrops that Perseverance might examine later.</p><p>&#34;We&#39;re hoping the color images will provide the closest look yet at &#39;Pilot Pinnacle,&#39; a location featuring outcrops that some team members think may record some of the deepest water environments in old Lake Jezero,&#34; NASA scientists <a href=\"https://mars.nasa.gov/technology/helicopter/status/314/flight-9-was-a-nail-biter-but-ingenuity-came-through-with-flying-colors/\">wrote in a recent blog post</a>.</p><p>It&#39;s possible, though, that Perseverance&#39;s tight schedule won&#39;t allow it to visit the rocks, &#34;so Ingenuity may offer the only opportunity to study these deposits in any detail,&#34; the scientists said.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a href='https://www.businessinsider.com/nasa-mars-ingenuity-helicopter-10th-flight-2021-7'>Business Insider</a></div><div management firm GMO.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Boston Globe/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Stocks worldwide are expensive and US stock valuations are at page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>Billionaire Jeremy Grantham&#39;s investment firm says stocks are too pricey, outlining their view as US equities sit at record highs, with the team offering their advice on how to navigate through what they call a global growth bubble. </p><p>&#34;Global equity markets rallied impressively in the quarter, pushing some year-to-date numbers into double-digit territory only halfway through the calendar year,&#34; said GMO in its <a href=\"https://www.gmo.com/americas/research-library/2q-2021-market-commentary/\">second-quarter outlook published this week</a>. Grantham, co-founder of investment firm Grantham, Mayo, van Otterloo &#38; Co., is a legendary investor who is <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/stock-market-outlook-indicators-lined-up-bubble-burst-jeremy-grantham-2021-5?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingestgest\"><strong>well known</strong></a> for <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/news/stocks/stock-market-outlook-investing-legend-jeremy-grantham-bubble-crazy-rally-2020-11-1029801632?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingestgest\"><strong>calling</strong></a> for a burst of the 1989 Japanese asset-price bubble, the 2000 tech bubble, and the 2008 real-estate bubble.</p><p>&#34;It gives us no pleasure to remind our clients that U.S. stock valuations, by almost any backward or forward-looking measure that we&#39;ve come up with, are at levels that concern us,&#34; said GMO&#39;s asset allocation team. &#34;If one must own U.S. stocks, however, as many institutions and advisors do, we suggest leaning into value and cyclicals while maintaining a quality bias.&#34; </p><p>The <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/index/s&p_500_500\">S&#38;P 500</a> index on Friday pushed its year-to-date advance to about 17%, overcoming a selloff this week. The benchmark has recently made a<a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/news/stocks/sp500-outlook-stock-market-record-highs-1995-stocks-bespoke-investment-2021-7\"> lengthy string of record highs</a> fueled by expectations of strong corporate earnings expansion although investors have run into economic growth concerns as COVID-19 cases continue to mount worldwide. </p><p>GMO noted that the MSCI EAFE Index -- tracking stocks in Europe, Asia and Australia -- has packed on nearly 9% this year and the MSCI EM Index gauging stocks in 27 emerging market countries, has risen by more than 7%. </p><p>The firm foresees negative returns on an inflation-adjusted basis over the next seven years for 10 of the 11 asset classes it monitors. It expects a negative return of 8% in US large-cap stocks and of 8.5% in US small-cap stocks. </p><p><em><strong>Read more:</strong> <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/stock-picks-to-buy-value-profit-revenue-growth-morgan-stanley-2021-7\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Morgan Stanley names 26 stocks outside the tech and media industries that have delivered market-beating growth in recent years - and look poised to continue that expansion in the future</a></em></p><p>&#34;Meanwhile, many among the Twitter-sphere and other social media discussions have expressed frustration with GMO&#39;s bearishness,&#34; the firm said, saying it has heard people wonder if it isn&#39;t giving enough credit to some high-growth &#34;disruptors&#34; with new business models. It said it does have models that take into account current optimistic growth forecasts. </p><p>&#34;Many individual companies are deserving of their current high multiples - we absolutely concede that somewhere in the global growth basket sits the next <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/stocks/amzn-stock\">Amazon</a>. Unfortunately, they&#39;re also ALL being priced that way, and for us, that is a bridge too far.&#34; </p><p>GMO said it has three actions it advises to clients traversing in a global growth bubble:</p><p><strong>1) exploit the bubble with an equity long/short strategy</strong></p><p><strong>2) avoid the bubble by investing in alternatives</strong></p><p><strong>3) concentrate assets away from the bubble in emerging market value, Japan small value, cyclicals, and quality. </strong></p><p>&#34;We are loath to recommend a traditional 60/40 mix. There will come a day when global equities and government bonds are fairly valued and should deliver a &#34;normal&#34; real rate of return,&#34; it said. On that day, GMO will be the first in line to tell you to own that traditional mix.&#34;</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a Marjorie Taylor Greene said that if she was in charge she would deport every Chinese person that was loyal to the Chinese Communist Party (CCP).</p><p>The Georgia politician made the comments while speaking to Steve Bannon on his &#34;War Room: Pandemic&#34; show, hosted by the fringe right-wing network Real America&#39;s Voice.</p><blockquote class=\"twitter-tweet blockquote\" data-lang=\"en\" data-cards=\"\" data-conversation=\"\">-PatriotTakes 🇺🇸  (@patriottakes) <a href=\"https://twitter.com/mims/statuses/1417855951055450114?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw\">July 21, 2021</a></blockquote><script async=\"\" src=\"//platform.twitter.com/widgets.js\" charset=\"utf-8\"></script><p> </p><p>&#34;If I was in charge and I had my way, I would come down on China so hard,&#34; Greene said. &#34;I would kick out every single Chinese in this country that is loyal to the CCP. They would be gone. I do not care who they are.&#34;</p><p>&#34;I don&#39;t care how much money you have, how much land you own, how many businesses you own, how much money you&#39;ve donated to colleges and universities,&#34; Greene said.</p><p>When Bannon asked whether this would apply to people like Chinese business magnate Jack Ma, Greene responded, &#34;If they are loyal to the CCP, they go back.&#34;</p><p>It&#39;s unclear how she would determine who was &#34;loyal&#34; to the Chinese Communist Party. </p><p>Greene added that the US should not do business with companies aligned with the Chinese Communist Party.</p><p>&#34;We should not be funding CCP companies,&#34; Greene said. &#34;We should not be doing business with an enemy that&#39;s trying to kill us, did kill us this past year.&#34;</p><p>Her comments allude to a conspiracy theory fuelled by top Republicans including Donald Trump that blamed China and Chinese people for spreading COVID-19.</p><p>The past year has seen a rise in hate crimes and harassment towards Asian Americans. Advocacy group Stop AAPI Hate said it received over <a href=\"https://secureservercdn.net/104.238.69.231/a1w.90d.myftpupload.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/02/Press-Statement-re_-Bay-Area-Elderly-Incidents-2.9.2021-1.pdf\">2,800 reports of hate incidents</a> directed at Asian Americans in 2020.</p><p>A <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/anti-defamation-league-study-donald-trump-anti-asian-hate-speech-2021-3?r=US&IR=TIR=T\">study</a> by the Anti-Defamation League also showed that the pandemic corresponded with a rise in hate speech and harassment on social networks aimed at Asian Americans.</p><p>Earlier this week Greene made similar comments on C-SPAN, saying she would not give student visas to anyone who was &#34;loyal to the CCP&#34;. She also questioned whether China had &#34;other bioweapons&#34; similar to the coronavirus and what they are &#34;planning to do with them.&#34;</p><blockquote class=\"twitter-tweet blockquote\" data-lang=\"en\" data-cards=\"\" data-conversation=\"\">-Rep. Shoaib)","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i.insider.com/60ae020fbee0fc0019d597e9?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn24@2020_6/2021/07/25/10-54-22-650_4241e5d83d4c9cf1.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn39@2020_3/2021/07/25/10-54-25-234_cc3ef43fc8ff5801.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=_Y8dcmIlxdE:SfvjyKYcVzA:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn24@2020_5/2021/07/25/10-54-24-544_e5b04a8d612b19e3.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=_Y8dcmIlxdE:SfvjyKYcVzA:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn16@2020_4/2021/07/25/10-54-22-756_47e84dd552999453.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn95@2020_6/2021/07/25/10-54-23-641_740defb435ea442b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=_Y8dcmIlxdE:SfvjyKYcVzA:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn28@2020_4/2021/07/25/10-54-24-570_8c964e9db7890187.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/_Y8dcmIlxdE":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn27@2020_2/2021/07/25/10-54-22-719_8dffc7988a3420f2.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1627210446775},{"createdTime":"2021-07-25 18:54:06","updatedTime":"2021-07-25 18:54:06","title":"A fully vaccinated mom caught COVID-19 after her kids went to summer camp: 'Our kids are Trojan-horsing us'","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/fully-vaccinated-mom-catches-covid-19-after-kids-summer-camp-2021-7","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/60f6d9329addaf00191eaf42?format=jpeg\" alt=\"hilary young in a hat on the beach with her two daughters, 6 and 3.\" height=\"2933\" width=\"2200\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Hilary Young (right) with her two daughters, who got sick after attending a summer camp. Young and her 6-year-old both tested positive for COVID-19.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Courtesy of Hilary Young</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>A fully vaccinated mom tested positive for COVID-19 after a counselor at her daughter&#39;s summer camp went home sick with the coronavirus.</li>\n<li>The mother developed cold-like symptoms, but never had a fever or shortness of breath.</li>\n<li>&#34;My symptoms seemed exactly like a cold,&#34; she said. &#34;Our kids are Trojan-horsing us. They are the way in.&#34;</li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>After more than a year of careful masking, distancing, and worrying about getting COVID-19, Hilary Young was fully vaccinated with mRNA shots, and ready to have some fun this summer with her family. </p><p>&#34;We were having a hot vax summer, and it got derailed,&#34; Young told Insider, after both she and her six-year-old daughter tested positive for COVID-19. </p><p>She suspects their illnesses could be part of a much wider outbreak, one that may have started at a summer camp. <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/covid-expert-vaccinated-people-can-spread-the-delta-variant-2021-7\">Anecdotal</a> <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/more-kids-hospitalized-covid-masks-vaccines-protect-children-2021-7\">data</a> <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/fully-vaccinated-people-got-covid-after-an-outdoor-wedding-2021-7\">suggests</a> there <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/fully-vaccinated-people-not-immune-to-covid-milder-2021-7\">may be many others like it</a> now that the <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/fully-vaccinated-people-not-immune-to-covid-milder-2021-7\">more infectious Delta variant is around</a>, and few vaccinated people are getting tested for their mild, cold-like symptoms.</p><p>&#34;All of us with young kids, that&#39;s a huge unvaccinated population,&#34; Young said of kids under 12 (who <a href=\"https://www.cdc.gov/coronavirus/2019-ncov/vaccines/recommendations/adolescents.html\">can&#39;t yet get vaccinated in the US</a>). &#34;I think our kids are Trojan-horsing us. They are the way in.&#34;</p><p>She considers her story a cautionary tale about the dangers of COVID-19 spreading among vaccinated adults.</p><p>&#34;My worst fear happened, we survived it, and I think that&#39;s a direct result of being vaccinated,&#34; Young, who lives in Philadelphia, Pennsylvania, said.  </p><h2>A trip to the beach, where fully vaccinated adults got sick</h2><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/60f70a94b9172800127521a9?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Beach houses pictured with sand dunes in Ocean City, New Jersey.\" height=\"1396\" width=\"1862\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>\n<p class=\"copyright\">John Greim/LightRocket via Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>The illnesses began after Young&#39;s daughters attended a summer camp the week before the July 4th weekend.</p><p>One of the girls&#39; counselors went home sick early on in the week. It wasn&#39;t until Friday that campers and their parents learned she&#39;d tested positive for COVID-19.</p><p>By then, it had already been three days, and Young&#39;s daughters weren&#39;t showing any symptoms, so they went ahead to join extended family at the beach for the holiday. </p><p>&#34;We figured, &#39;OK, even if the kids are sick, we&#39;ll all be fine, it&#39;ll be fine, we&#39;re all vaccinated,&#39;&#34; she said.</p><p>All weekend, her three-year-old was &#34;crazy cranky.&#34;</p><p>&#34;Lots of meltdowns, lots of tantrums, very tired, but no discernible symptoms,&#34; she added. </p><p>By Monday evening, her six-year-old felt fatigued. She later developed a low-grade fever, headache, runny nose, and some nausea.</p><p>Some older adults in the beach house started feeling unwell too, with fatigue, and sore throats. </p><p>&#34;There were six [fully] vaccinated adults in the house,&#34; Young said. &#34;Four of us felt symptoms.&#34; </p><p>Young got a lab test for her six-year-old at a pharmacy, which came back positive for COVID-19.</p><p>&#34;We have tried so hard for the last year and a half to keep our kids safe,&#34; she said. &#34;I think everyone was very complacent, happy to have their lives back, feeling like this is over.&#34;</p><h2>More like a cold than COVID-19</h2><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/60f70ac5b9172800127521ce?format=jpeg\" alt=\"sinus/cold\" height=\"2283\" width=\"3044\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Sinus infections last longer than colds, last longer, and require antibiotics.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Halfpoint Images/ Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>Young did an at-home BinaxNOW rapid test on herself once she developed symptoms.</p><p>&#34;I just started sobbing because it was like, &#39;oh my God, I have, COVID, I can&#39;t believe this happening,&#39;&#34; she said, phoning her doctor.</p><p>&#34;I was like, &#39;what should I do?&#39; And she was like, &#39;nothing ... <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/what-to-do-if-vaccinated-and-exposed-to-covid-experts-2021-7\">you just have to quarantine</a> and you should be fine.&#39;&#34;</p><p>For the vaccinated, <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/covid-19-delta-variant-symptoms-headache-runny-nose-sore-throat-2021-6\">COVID-19 can appear like a mild illness</a>. </p><p>Young&#39;s symptoms started with a sore throat, then &#34;full-on congestion&#34; and some dizziness. She said she used NyQuil to sleep for about a week, and Tylenol Cold &#38; Sinus to relieve the stuffiness.</p><p>&#34;I lost my sense of taste and smell, which is just starting to come back a little bit now,&#34; she added, but &#34;I never had chills. I never had any respiratory issues.&#34;</p><p>&#34;It wasn&#39;t that bad for me,&#34; Young added, sharing that some of her unvaccinated friends believe her story is another reason why they don&#39;t need to get shots. She could not disagree more. </p><p>&#34;I billed out 25 hours to clients last week,&#34; the branding consultant said. &#34;Despite not feeling well, I was functioning. I <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/cdc-director-says-covid-19-hospitalizations-are-mostly-unvaccinated-2021-7\">don&#39;t think it will be that kind</a> to unvaccinated people.&#34; </p><h2>What we know about kids and COVID transmission</h2><p>Though it&#39;s hard to know for sure exactly how she and her daughter caught COVID-19, Young says it&#39;s not hard to imagine how the pathogen might&#39;ve traveled from the camp, through the girls, and then to the vaccinated elders in their beach house. </p><p>&#34;I don&#39;t know if you have kids, but my kids are just constantly in my face, there&#39;s no personal space,&#34; she said.</p><p>Early <a href=\"https://virological.org/t/viral-infection-and-transmission-in-a-large-well-traced-outbreak-caused-by-the-delta-sars-cov-2-variant/724\">research suggests</a> it may be easier to catch COVID-19 from unvaccinated kids <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/delta-variant-new-covid-19-cases-cdc-director-2021-7\">now</a> than it was months ago<a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/delta-variant-new-covid-19-cases-cdc-director-2021-7\"></a>. One <a href=\"https://www.npr.org/sections/goatsandsoda/2021/07/08/1013794996/why-the-delta-variant-is-so-contagious-a-new-study-sheds-light\">Chinese preprint study</a> found the viral load of people with Delta is about 1,000 times higher than earlier versions of the virus. </p><p>Delta &#34;can glom on stronger, and cause infection easier,&#34; Dr. Hilary Babcock, medical director of infection prevention at Barnes-Jewish and St. Louis Children&#39;s Hospitals in Missouri, said. Babcock said some fully vaccinated staff at her hospital are getting infected through their kids, too.</p><p>&#34;People who are infected are spreading more virus around them, because they have more virus in their respiratory tract,&#34; she added. </p><p>However, the same disease prevention measures <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/more-kids-hospitalized-covid-masks-vaccines-protect-children-2021-7\">many educators adopted last year</a> can still work to <a href=\"https://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/volumes/70/wr/mm7012e3.htm\">crush transmission in the classroom.</a> <a href=\"http://modmedmicro.nsms.ox.ac.uk/wp-content/uploads/2021/07/dct_schools_trial_preprint_20210722.pdf\">One recent UK preprint even suggests</a> that daily, rapid testing of students can work just as well as isolation at preventing future spread in schools.</p><h2>The CDC is &#39;not going to have the right data&#39;</h2><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/60f5f1e39addaf00191eae36?format=jpeg\" alt=\"man in a mask helps a kid roll in an inflatable trampoline during COVID-19 pandemic\" height=\"1496\" width=\"2100\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Summer camp in Culver City, California on June 14, 2021.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Genaro Molina / Los Angeles Times via Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>Young wanted to let the CDC know about her breakthrough case, but found there was nowhere to report it to the agency. (The CDC is only keeping <a href=\"https://www.cdc.gov/vaccines/covid-19/health-departments/breakthrough-cases.html\">close tabs on severe breakthrough cases</a>, those requiring hospitalization.)</p><p>&#34;My symptoms seemed exactly like a cold,&#34; Young said. &#34;If we hadn&#39;t known about my daughter&#39;s counselor testing positive, we never would have assumed it was COVID. We never would&#39;ve gotten tested. And we would have gone out into the world.&#34;</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a 18:54:06","updatedTime":"2021-07-25 18:54:06","title":"Why China's crypto crackdown is creating 'tremendous' opportunities for miners in other countries, according to industry execs","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/china-bitcoin-mining-ban-riot-blockchain-renewable-jason-les-crackdown-2021-7","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/60fb1efb0729770012b9b14d?format=jpeg\" alt=\"GettyImages 1020194172\" height=\"1499\" width=\"3000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>\n<p class=\"copyright\">Olga Maltseva/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Insider spoke to two bitcoin mining industry executives about the state of the industry after China&#39;s crackdown. </li>\n<li>The drop in bitcoin&#39;s hashrate as a result of the ban has made mining more profitable and easier than ever, Riot Blockchain CEO said. </li>\n<li>US mining companies are also facing a flood of demand from Chinese miners looking to house mining operations.</li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\"></a><a href=\"https://newsletter.businessinsider.com/join/4np/10-things-opening-bell?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingest?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingestm=ingest\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><strong>Sign up here for our daily newsletter, 10 Things Before the Opening Bell.</strong></a></li>\n</ul><p>China has taken a harsh view of the crypto space since the early days of bitcoin. Industry experts say though that June&#39;s outright <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/currencies/news/bitcoin-price-btc-china-mining-crackdown-sichuan-cryptocurrencies-ether-2021-6\">ban on mining operations</a> has been one of the most impactful moves by the country on the bitcoin network. </p><p>Look no further than the chart of the bitcoin hashrate, the measure of computing power being contributed to the network through mining, to see the ban&#39;s effect: a significant drop-off beginning in June as the authorities in the country implemented the ban. </p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/60fb20c9fc253600182039d5?format=jpeg\" alt=\"hashrate\" height=\"389\" width=\"526\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>\n<p class=\"copyright\">The Block Crypto, CoinMetrics</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p> </p><p>The drop off isn&#39;t entirely negative for the mining industry, however. In fact, as more miners suddenly shut down in China, it made mining easier, and more profitable for those still able to operate.</p><p>&#34;As miners, we&#39;re all competing against each other for the same fixed piece of pie, so when the competition drops off like that, you&#39;re just getting a bigger piece of the pie,&#34; said Jason Les, CEO of <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/stock/RIOT\">Riot Blockchain</a>, one of the largest publicly traded mining companies. <br><br>He described to Insider how the network hashrate dropped 50% within a matter of weeks in June as mining in China stopped. Due to how bitcoin was designed, a drop in the hash rate also led to a drop in the network difficulty, meaning it was easier to mine with less people competing, and subsequently more profitable.</p><p>Les said the crackdown and drop in hashrate has created &#34;tremendous&#34; opportunities for miners unaffected by the ban, though he deeply sympathizes with miners in China, which is the birthplace of the bitcoin mining industry. </p><p>&#34;I can&#39;t imagine going through something like that, where just suddenly you&#39;ve devoted so much time and capital, you built this business, and then at the stroke of a pen, the government decides your business is done,&#34; he said. </p><p>As a result of the ban, miners in China are scrambling to relocate their operations to other countries. Les said his Linkedin inbox is flooded with inquiries from miners looking for facility space in the US.</p><p>&#34;There have been Chinese miners who have just showed up in Texas asking &#39;can you take us to a mining facility that you know is nearby? We need capacity,&#34; he added. </p><p>The migration out of China is especially good for mining companies that are able to host third-party miners within their facilities, like Riot.</p><p>&#34;Being a hoster puts us in a great position because now we have an increased base of prospective customers looking for space,&#34; said Les. </p><p>Interestingly, the mining ban in China has also helped bitcoin toward a fundamental goal of becoming a truly decentralized network, Whit Gibbs, CEO and co-founder of Compass Mining, told Insider. </p><p>Gibbs said the most &#34;dangerous thing for Bitcoin&#34; is for more than 50% of the hash rate to be located in any one country and vulnerable to the whims of one country&#39;s regulations. </p><p>For many years, many bitcoin enthusiasts were concerned about the hashrate being heavily concentrated in China. Now, miners are flocking to countries like the US, Kazakhstan, and Russia.</p><p>According to the <a href=\"https://www.jbs.cam.ac.uk/insight/2021/new-data-reveals-timeline-of-chinas-bitcoin-mining-exodus/\">Cambridge Centre for Alternative Finance</a> (CCAF), the US&#39;s share of the total <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/currencies/btc-usd?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingestgest\">bitcoin</a> hashrate increased from a mere 4.6% to 16.8% from September 2019 to April 2021. </p><p>&#34;It makes for a more secure Bitcoin,&#34; Gibbs said.</p><p> </p><p> </p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a 18:54:06","updatedTime":"2021-07-25 18:54:06","title":"California Gov. Gavin Newsom signs bill that bans police from posting mugshots of people accused of non-violent crimes on social media","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/newsom-signs-bill-that-bans-police-from-posting-mugshots-2021-7","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/60fc4ffb28e2cf0018905c79?format=jpeg\" alt=\"California Gov. Gavin Newsom\" height=\"1848\" width=\"3696\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>California Governor Gavin Newsom at a June 2021 press conference.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Alberto E. Rodriguez/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>California Gov. Gavin Newsom on Friday signed a bill preventing police from sharing mugshots on social media of individuals accused of non-violent crimes. </li>\n<li>The bill&#39;s sponsor said he hoped the legislation helps to tackle &#34;unconscious bias,&#34; according to the <a href=\"https://www.sfchronicle.com/crime/article/Bay-Area-lawmaker-wants-to-keep-mugshots-of-16335621.php\">San Francisco Chronicle</a>. </li>\n<li>Under the law, police can still share photos of individuals they believe are dangerous who those who are accused of violent crimes.</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>California Gov. Gavin Newsom on Friday signed into law a bill that bans police from sharing on social media the mugshots of individuals charged with non-violent offenses. </p><p>Assembly Member Evan Low, a Democrat representing San Jose, sponsored the bill, <a href=\"https://leginfo.legislature.ca.gov/faces/billVotesClient.xhtml?bill_id=202120220AB1475\">known as AB 1475</a>, and told the <a href=\"https://www.sfchronicle.com/crime/article/Bay-Area-lawmaker-wants-to-keep-mugshots-of-16335621.php\">San Francisco Chronicle</a> that he authored the legislation to reduce &#34;unconscious bias&#34;  and &#34;the assumption of guilt that&#34; is created when police post booking photos online.</p><p>Sometimes, he noted, police will share photos mocking those accused of crimes.</p><p>The bill passed with bipartisan support, and there was no organized campaign against the legislation, the Chronicle reported. The law also passed with the support of San Francisco Police Chief Bill Scott, Low said, according to The Chronicle.</p><p>&#34;In recent years, many departments have used their social media accounts to shame suspects arrested by officers, posting mug shots, names and alleged crimes,&#34; Low wrote in a letter to Newsom that urged him to sign the bill, according to the Chronicle.</p><p>&#34;The publication of these mugshots does little to serve a legitimate public safety interest as suspects are already in custody,&#34; he added.</p><p>The new law still allows law enforcement to post the mugshots of criminals accused of violent crimes, fugitives, or individuals that police believe pose an immediate threat to the public.</p><p>The law also creates a way for people who have been accused of non-violent crimes to have their photos removed from social media pages of police departments that already shared them on social media, according to the legislation. Those accused of violent crimes can also request their mugshot be removed should they meet criteria set forth in the legislation.</p><p> </p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on src=\"https://i.insider.com/60f9def3fc253600181ff3eb?format=jpeg\" alt=\"walmart shopper\" height=\"623\" width=\"1247\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>\n<p class=\"copyright\">REUTERS/ Kevork Djansezian</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Walmart has more than 3,500 Supercenters across the US and made $555 billion in sales last year.</li>\n<li>The retail giant&#39;s size and reach means the typical Walmart shopper closely resembles the average US consumer.</li>\n<li>Walmart&#39;s average customer is a woman who is 59.5 years old, white, married, and earning $80,000 per year.</li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>Walmart is the largest retailer in the world, pulling in more than $555 billion in sales last year. Each week, 220 million customers visit its stores across 24 countries.</p><p>In the US, the company has more than 5,000 locations, including 3,570 Supercenters, and it employs nearly 1.6 million people.</p><p>Because of the retail giant&#39;s scale, the average Walmart shopper has a lot in common with the average US consumer, according to data from the <a href=\"https://www.numerator.com/\">analytics firm Numerator</a> prepared for Insider.</p><p>Roughly 95% of the shoppers in Numerator&#39;s panel visited Walmart two or more times in the past year.</p><p>Walmart&#39;s typical shopper in the US is a white woman between 55 and 64 years old, who is married and living in the suburbs of the Southeast. She typically has an undergraduate degree and earns about $80,000 per year.</p><p>She visits Walmart at least once per week - about 63 trips per year - and picks up 13 products for a total cost of about $54 per trip. 13.5% of her spending takes place at Walmart, while she spends about 11% at Amazon.</p><p>Her primary shopping categories in-store are groceries, including chicken, fruit, snacks and sweets, but she also gets a lot of fast food. Her favorite five brands at Walmart are Turkey Knob, Cheetos, Betty Crocker, Dole, and Tyson.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a consistently beaten Wall Street estimates in the second quarter.\n<p class=\"copyright\">JOHANNES EISELE/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>The S&#38;P 500 is on track for its best quarterly earnings growth since 2009, data shows.</li>\n<li>Companies are smashing expectations, with 88% so far outstripping estimates in Q2.</li>\n<li>Strong earnings are supporting stocks, but the delta COVID-19 variant remains a risk.</li>\n<li aria-level=\"1\"><a href=\"https://newsletter.businessinsider.com/join/4np/10-things-opening-bell?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingest?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingest?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingest?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingest?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingest?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingestts&#38;utm_medium=ingest\" 500</a> companies are on track for the best earnings growth since 2009, with companies smashing estimates as the US economy bounces back to life from COVID-19 lockdowns.</p><p>The strong quarter is helping support stocks despite high equity prices, <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/consumer-price-index-inflation-june-2021-7\">rising inflation,</a> and the threat of the <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/delta-variant-new-covid-19-cases-cdc-director-2021-7\">delta coronavirus variant</a>.</p><p>Earnings for S&#38;P 500 companies are expected to grow 78.1% year-on-year in the second quarter, according to figures from financial data company Refinitiv, released Friday. That would be the best quarterly performance since the final three months of 2009, during the initial rebound from the financial crisis.</p><p>Around a quarter of the 500 companies that make up the benchmark US stock index have reported. Those in the industrial, consumer discretionary, and financial sectors are set to do the best as they profit from the reopening of the economy and favorable comparisons to last year&#39;s weak second-quarter earnings.</p><p>So far, 88% of S&#38;P 500 companies have beaten earnings per share estimates for the second quarter, according to data provider FactSet.</p><p>The strong results put the S&#38;P 500 on track to be the best quarter for beats since FactSet started tracking the data in 2008. The current record was registered in the first quarter of 2021, when 86% of companies beat estimates.</p><p><em><strong>Read more:</strong> <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/stocks-to-buy-goldman-sachs-minimal-risk-adjusted-high-returns-2021-7\">GOLDMAN SACHS: 33 stocks to buy right now for strong returns of at least 15% and minimal risk as the economic reopening helps equities grind higher into year-end</a></em></p><p>And companies are raising their expectations for earnings and sales as the economic rebound continues. For example, <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/stocks/ko-stock\">Coca-Cola</a> stock jumped on Wednesday when it <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/news/stocks/coca-cola-beats-expectations--raises-guidance-10354842\">increased its revenue and earnings outlook</a>, citing the strong economy.</p><p>Investors have not reacted strongly to the second-quarter earnings, given that the S&#38;P 500 has already soared more than 90% since its pandemic-induced crash in March 2020.</p><p>But the strong earnings are helping the benchmark US stock index hover at record highs.</p><p>JPMorgan analysts, led by Dubravko Lakos-Bujas, on Tuesday upgraded their year-end target for the S&#38;P 500 from 4,400 to 4,600, which would be roughly a 5% increase from Friday&#39;s level.</p><p>The index &#34;should be supported by strong earnings growth and capital return until 2023,&#34; Lakos-Bujas and colleagues said in a note.</p><p>Importantly for investors, profit margins are strong despite rising inflation and some reports of higher costs. John Butters, senior earnings analyst at FactSet, said net profit margin for the S&#38;P 500 for the second quarter is expected to be 12.4%.</p><p>&#34;If 12.4% is the actual net profit margin for the quarter, it will mark the second-highest net profit margin for the index since FactSet began tracking this metric in 2008, trailing only last quarter&#39;s net profit margin of 12.8%,&#34; he said.</p><p>Yet, there are risks ahead, not least the fact that the coronavirus pandemic is far from over. Delta variant cases have soared in Britain, and survey data suggests the numbers are <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/business/2021/jul/23/uk-recovery-slows-amid-waning-consumer-demand-and-staff-shortages\">slowing the country&#39;s economic recovery</a>.</p><p>Cases are now on the rise again in the US, contributing in part to a <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/news/stocks/stock-market-news-today-dow-falls-delta-variant-covid-economy-2021-7\">sharp sell-off on Monday</a>, which saw the S&#38;P 500 drop 1.6% and the <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/index/dow_jones\">Dow Jones</a> fall 2.1%.</p><p>However, many investors are hoping high vaccination rates can keep advanced economies ticking over. Mark Haefele, chief investment officer at UBS Global Wealth Management, said in a note: &#34;We see reasons to look beyond the delta variant headlines and stay risk-on, with a tilt toward reflation and reopening beneficiaries.&#34;</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a Page still holds majority voting control, along with cofounder Sergey Brin, of a company worth $1.8 trillion. So it&#39;s not unreasonable to wonder what he&#39;s up to. </p><p><strong><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/larry-page-google-bought-fiji-island-pandemic-hiding-tavarua-namotu-2021-7\">Insider reporter Hugh Langley tracked Page down to Fiji</a>,</strong> where he&#39;s been spotted by locals on two of the archipelago&#39;s islands over the past year. There are even rumors that he bought an island (Fiji has 300 of them).</p><p>While Fiji has been closed to tourists during the pandemic, Page apparently entered through the country&#39;s special &#34;Blue Lane&#34; initiative which offers passage to superyacht and private jet owners who abide by quarantine rules. </p><p><strong>Is Page hiding out in the South Pacific for the duration of the pandemic, or has he decided to relocate there permanently?</strong></p><ul><li>The fact that Page also <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/google-cofounder-larry-page-sold-superyacht-senses-fiji-yacht-billionaire-2021-7\">sold his $45 million superyacht</a> provides fodder for speculation about a Hernan Cortes-moment (the 16th century conquistador famously burned, or sunk, depending on the telling, his ships to force his troops to march inland towards Tenochtitlan).</li><li>Longtime Google watchers will also surely recall that Page once famously mused about <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/larry-page-google-alphabet-last-big-speech-future-hints-2019-12\">setting aside part of the world for techies to have a &#34;safe&#34; space</a> to do experiments and escape &#34;the negativity&#34; of regulators and the media. </li></ul><p><strong>Potential takeaway:</strong> If you thought <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/why-miami-mayor-is-worried-about-tech-vc-startup-scene\">Miami was the new destination for techies seeking to build utopia</a>, you might want to book a ticket to Fiji instead - if you can get in.</p><p><strong>Read our full report here:</strong></p><ul><li><strong><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/larry-page-google-bought-fiji-island-pandemic-hiding-tavarua-namotu-2021-7\">Reclusive billionaire and Google cofounder Larry Page has been off the grid for over a year. Sources say he&#39;s been hiding out on Fijian islands that are cut off from most travelers during the pandemic.</a></strong></li></ul><hr><h3>Netflix, the game!</h3><p><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/top-netflix-leaders-of-product-and-operations-teams-greg-peters-2021-7\"><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5fa17ff069331a0011bc6f4f?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Reed Hastings\" height=\"1378\" width=\"1838\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>\n<p class=\"copyright\">Ernesto S. Ruscio/Getty Images / Netflix</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure></a></p><p>What&#39;s the answer to an increasingly crowded video streaming market? <strong><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/top-netflix-leaders-of-product-and-operations-teams-greg-peters-2021-7\">If you&#39;re Netflix, it&#39;s video games. </a></strong></p><ul><li>During its earnings call this week, the company said its lost 400,000 subscribers in the US and Canada, but <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/netflix-gaming-plans-marketing-device-for-engagement-2021-7\">confirmed plans to start offering video games</a> to subscribers at no extra cost.</li><li>The games will, at least initially, be designed for users&#39; smartphones rather than for televisions or PCs. As for the games themselves, <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/bridgerton-compared-to-other-netflix-top-original-shows-viewership-2021-1\">Netflix has a nice catalog of original content</a> like Stranger Things and Lupin that could be spun into games - these <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/netflix-franchise-building-will-be-essential-to-video-game-plans-2021-7\">franchises will be an important ingredient in Netflix&#39;s foray into games.</a></li><li>It&#39;s still early, but it will be interesting to watch if this is the first sign of Netflix <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/how-much-is-amazon-prime\">taking a page from Amazon Prime</a> and giving members a broader &#34;basket&#34; of offerings for the price of one monthly subscription.</li><li>Amazon, meanwhile, is getting some static on its video game efforts. According to some user reports on Reddit  Amazon&#39;s latest online game <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/amazon-new-world-rtx-3090-graphics-card-brick-2021-7\">&#34;New World&#34; is destroying their graphics cards</a> - and not just any graphics cards, these are $1500 Nvidia RTX 3090 graphics cards. Game over. </li></ul><hr><h3>The Big Read</h3><h3><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/inside-wework-ipo-meltdown-bankers-adam-neumann-chaotic-partnership-2021-7\">Ice baths, Bob Marley lyrics and billion dollar loans: How Adam Neumann played Wall Street&#39;s top bankers off of each other in the run up to WeWork&#39;s doomed IPO</a></h3><p><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/inside-wework-ipo-meltdown-bankers-adam-neumann-chaotic-partnership-2021-7\"><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5d31d93236e03c26be0917aa?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Ice bath\" height=\"2021\" width=\"2695\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>\n<p class=\"copyright\">Alexandre Simoes/Borussia Dortmund/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure></a></p><p>The epic, cautionary tale of WeWork&#39;s 2019 IPO debacle is in the news again thanks to the new book, <a href=\"https://www.penguinrandomhouse.com/books/645810/the-cult-of-we-by-eliot-brown-and-maureen-farrell/\">&#34;The Cult of We,&#34;</a> by Wall Street Journal reporters Maureen Farrell and Eliot Brown.</p><p><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/inside-wework-ipo-meltdown-bankers-adam-neumann-chaotic-partnership-2021-7\">In an exclusive adaptation from the book written for Insider</a>, the two journalists home in on the chaotic relationship between WeWork founder Adam Neumann and the banks competing to win the &#34;lead left&#34; role overseeing the IPO. It&#39;s a surreal and eye-opening story that will make you look differently at the way buzzy startups get listed on the public markets.</p><h3><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/inside-wework-ipo-meltdown-bankers-adam-neumann-chaotic-partnership-2021-7\">Click here to read the full story.</a></h3><ul><li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/goldman-sachs-amazing-wework-ipo-pitch-deck-adam-neumann-2021-7\">And don&#39;t miss the amazing pitch book that Goldman Sachs created to woo Adam Neumann.</a></li></ul><hr><h3><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/twitter-jack-dorsey-bitcoin-community-weird-as-hell-inspiration-2021-7\">Quote of the week:</a></h3><p><a 2021 in Miami, Florida.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Joe Raedle/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure></a></p><h3><em>&#34;It&#39;s deeply principled, it&#39;s weird as hell, it&#39;s always evolving.&#34;</em></h3><p><strong>- Jack Dorsey, the CEO of Twitter and of Square,</strong> <a the internet when he was a kid.</p><hr><h3>Recommended readings: </h3><p><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/uber-losing-employees-high-turnover-attrition-rate-hr-chief-problem-2021-7\">Uber is (Alexei Oreskovic)","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i.insider.com/55ad5c0add0895c4368b46f1?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn20@2020_6/2021/07/25/10-54-25-163_b05889cd07f602bd.webp","https://i.insider.com/5fa17ff069331a0011bc6f4f?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn76@2020_5/2021/07/25/10-54-23-132_4c4626bc59c8e4cb.webp","https://i.insider.com/5d31d93236e03c26be0917aa?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn100@2020_4/2021/07/25/10-54-24-089_bd8ec4241bc1626a.webp","https://i.insider.com/60f938a80729770012b9369f?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn43@2020_5/2021/07/25/10-54-24-206_ac7293358686665a.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn39@2020_3/2021/07/25/10-54-25-234_cc3ef43fc8ff5801.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=E7ceDsr3-Bs:I72z1qqo91s:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn63@2020_5/2021/07/25/10-54-24-004_42af73cf46dbdde3.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=E7ceDsr3-Bs:I72z1qqo91s:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn92@2020_4/2021/07/25/10-54-25-358_a11f9376bc518864.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn95@2020_6/2021/07/25/10-54-23-641_740defb435ea442b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=E7ceDsr3-Bs:I72z1qqo91s:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn3@2020_6/2021/07/25/10-54-23-023_d057c1a3bee22c66.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/E7ceDsr3-Bs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn100@2020_3/2021/07/25/10-54-23-228_efb47ce7e2c909df.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1627210446766},{"createdTime":"2021-07-25 18:54:06","updatedTime":"2021-07-25 18:54:06","title":"Heatwaves are killing workers on the job. The government needs to set new rules for employers before more Americans needlessly die.","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/covid-19-pandemic-heatwaves-federal-response-workers-farmers-2021-7","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/60fad76ffc2536001820115d?format=jpeg\" alt=\"A man wipes sweat from his face in sweltering heat.\" height=\"2384\" width=\"4768\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>A man wipes sweat from his face in sweltering heat.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Getty</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li aria-level=\"1\">Extreme heat is a public health crisis, just like the coronavirus pandemic.</li>\n<li aria-level=\"1\">Essential workers and communities of color are put at higher risk for heat-related illness and lack protections, just as they have during the pandemic.</li>\n<li aria-level=\"1\">While emissions need to be cut, the present impacts of heat on people need to be addressed with changes to regulations and infrastructure.</li>\n<li>Abdullah Shihipar is a writer who covers public health, class, and race.</li>\n<li>This is an opinion column. The thoughts expressed are those of the author.</li>\n</ul><p>The heatwave that swept across the West Coast of the US over the past month brought temperatures of over 110 degrees to the West coast and resulted in devastating consequences - mostly notably causing  <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2021/07/09/us/heat-wave-deaths.html\">more than 200 deaths</a>. </p><p>Among those that died were many workers, forced to work through the brutal heat. One of those people who died was 51-year-old<a href=\"https://www.kgw.com/article/news/local/oregon-osha-death-investigation-walmart-warehouse-worker-hermiston/283-4a855197-a1f7-4259-85b4-265b82ccb1f4\"> Kenton Scott Krupp, a worker at the </a> Walmart distribution center in Hermiston, Oregon.</p><p>According to the New York Times, <a href=\"https://www.kgw.com/article/news/local/oregon-osha-death-investigation-walmart-warehouse-worker-hermiston/283-4a855197-a1f7-4259-85b4-265b82ccb1f4\">Krupp</a> worked in a<a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2021/07/09/us/heat-wave-deaths.html\"> hot trailer</a> with only a fan to circulate air, and prior to the end of his shift he found it hard to speak and walk. </p><p>Two days after Krupp&#39;s death, Sebastian Francisco Perez, a migrant farm worker from Guatemala,<a href=\"https://apnews.com/article/canada-lifestyle-heat-waves-coronavirus-pandemic-immigration-ec7c31c85db5ef51e56e9d2fed33e719\"> died</a> from heat-related causes working on an apple orchard. These deaths expose the terrible conditions workers face while trying to earn a living.</p><p>Just like the COVID-19 crisis, workers were left to fend for themselves during the extreme heat - especially among low-income and minority communities. We were foolish not to tackle the pandemic early enough, and we can&#39;t afford to ignore that mistake when it comes to the all but guaranteed heatwaves of the future.</p><h2>Heat warning </h2><p>Upon prolonged exposure to extreme temperatures, people can develop heat exhaustion which can lead to headaches, dizziness, nausea and other debilitating symptoms. More seriously, one can develop heatstroke and begin to fall unconscious - which if left untreated, can lead to death. From making asthma worse, to making mental health worse, extreme heat can do untold<a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/weather-in-summer-heat-impact-on-health-2018-7#heat-waves-are-the-deadliest-form-of-extreme-weatherresponsible-for-more-deaths-in-the-us-every-year-than-the-combined-effects-of-hurricanes-lightning-tornadoes-earthquakes-and-floods-8\"> damage to our bodies</a>. Multiply this at a population level and you&#39;ve got a seasonal public health crisis.</p><p>Between<a href=\"https://www.ufwfoundation.org/federalheatprotections\"> 1992 and 2017</a>, 815 workers died from heat related causes and more than 70,000 were injured. New research published recently <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/us-news/2021/jul/21/us-worker-workplace-injury-heat-study-data\">has suggested</a> that this number may be a significant undercount. Farm workers working all day, outdoors, and with little to shade them are the most at risk. Statistics collected by the CDC from 1992 through 2006 show that US farm workers face a rate of death from heatstroke that is<a href=\"https://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/preview/mmwrhtml/mm5724a1.htm\"> 20 times higher</a> than other workers. During this heat, many workers have started their days under the cover of darkness at 2 or 3 AM.</p><p>Recognizing the danger the heat posed to farm workers, the United Farm Workers and other organizations have consistently called for regulations to protect workers from the heat.<a href=\"https://www.ufwfoundation.org/federalheatprotections\"> These include</a> providing breaks, water, access to shade and training on how to recognize heat stroke symptoms. On July 6, Oregon&#39;s governor<a href=\"https://www.oregon.gov/newsroom/Pages/NewsDetail.aspx?newsid=64091\"> directed</a> the state&#39;s Occupational Safety and Health Association (OSHA) to enact emergency rules that would protect workers from the heat. Washington instituted<a href=\"https://www.seattletimes.com/business/agriculture/washington-state-to-release-emergency-heat-rules-for-farmworkers-as-advocates-demand-added-protections/\"> similar emergency rules</a> days later. These changes are necessary, but they only came after workers died.</p><p>Farm workers may be the most at risk, but other workers have been harmed by the brutal heat. Last month, workers<a href=\"https://www.huffpost.com/entry/heat-wave-workers-walk-out_n_60dcd0c9e4b03d4073e55032?6ky\"> walked off the job</a> at restaurants across the country because of overheated working conditions . </p><p>These stories make it clear that workers - from construction workers, to line cooks, to warehouse workers - need a national standard to mitigate the risk for heat stroke across our rapidly warming country.</p><p>But instead of being proactive, the lack of a unified response to this public health crisis is similar to how the federal government approached the pandemic. <a href=\"https://www.pbs.org/wgbh/frontline/article/covid-19-farmworkers-among-highest-risk-studies-show/\">Farmworkers</a> and<a href=\"https://www.cnbc.com/2021/02/02/jobs-where-workers-have-the-highest-risk-of-dying-from-covid-study.html\"> line cooks</a> were also at higher risk for contracting and dying from COVID and  workers across industries were abandoned without any proper protections. When President Biden took office, he promised an emergency temporary standard that would address COVID. When a regulation was finally issued, it only<a href=\"https://news.bloomberglaw.com/daily-labor-report/osha-covid-19-rule-intended-to-cover-all-workers-draft-shows\"> covered healthcare workers</a>.</p><p>When we&#39;re talking about health, we can&#39;t ignore the role that our living environments play. With COVID, communities of color across the country saw<a href=\"https://www.washingtonpost.com/graphics/2020/investigations/coronavirus-race-data-map/\"> higher rates of the disease</a>, as a lot of this spread was<a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC7436547/\"> tied to work</a>. People would have to work in person and come home, often to<a href=\"https://khn.org/news/multigenerational-households-social-distancing-at-home-coronavirus/\"> multigenerational homes</a>, increasing the spread of the virus.It&#39;s a similar case for heat related illness. In 1995, when a blistering heatwave hit Chicago, most of the dead were<a href=\"https://www.nbcnews.com/news/us-news/heat-waves-fall-hardest-poor-elderly-experts-say-n1031871\"> low-income and Black residents of the city</a> who lived in apartments that did not have air conditioning. 25 years later, there isn&#39;t much that&#39;s changed.</p><p>According to statistics collected by the federal government over a decade ago,<a href=\"https://www.eia.gov/consumption/residential/reports/2009/air-conditioning.php\"> nearly 18% of households</a> below the poverty line do not have any form of air conditioning at all and about 30% of  households use window units or &#34;room air conditioning&#34; compared to central A/C. Of course, even if one has an air conditioner,using it can cause<a href=\"https://phys.org/news/2020-06-air-conditioner-electric-bill-energy.html\"> electric bills to increase</a> significantly, meaning some may have units but can&#39;t afford to use them. Extreme heat is especially a concern during the pandemic, when people may feel that it&#39;s unsafe to use communal<a href=\"https://www.bostonglobe.com/2020/07/09/metro/will-staying-cool-this-summer-be-luxury-only-some-can-afford/\"> public cooling centers</a>.</p><p>Of course, it&#39;s not just A/C. Low-income Black and Latinx communities are located in areas that are less likely to have<a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2021/06/30/opinion/environmental-inequity-trees-critical-infrastructure.html\"> tree cover</a>, and as a result, are more likely to be<a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2019/08/09/climate/city-heat-islands.html\"> heat islands</a> that attract heat - a legacy of racist<a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2020/08/24/climate/racism-redlining-cities-global-warming.html\"> redlining housing policies</a>.</p><p>All of this results in the same disparities that we saw with COVID.<a href=\"https://www.nbcnews.com/news/asian-america/heat-related-illness-affects-ethnic-groups-disproportionately-n976501\"> Data from California</a> shows that emergency room visits for heat-related illnesses increased for all groups between 2005 and 2015, but mostly increased for African Americans (67%), Hispanics (63%), Asian Americans (53%), as compared to white Americans (27%).<a href=\"https://www.reuters.com/article/us-temperature-immigrants-casualties/immigrant-workers-in-u-s-have-tripled-risk-for-heat-related-death-idUSKBN1DE2G3\"> Data collected by the CDC</a> shows that compared to US citizens, immigrants have nearly three times the risk of dying from a heat related illness.</p><p>So what do we do about it? In most states, there are<a href=\"https://www.apartments.com/blog/is-my-landlord-required-to-provide-heat-and-ac\"> no regulations</a> that require a landlord to provide air conditioning to renters - requiring that landlords provide cooling like they do heat is an easy start. Some rightfully point out that<a href=\"https://www.rollingstone.com/culture/culture-features/heat-waves-2021-western-us-climate-change-1187805/\"> A/C uses a lot of energy</a> and thereby contributes to the climate crisis, this is undoubtedly true but cooling is a necessity, not a luxury.</p><p>Of course, we can take steps to make neighborhoods cooler - by planting more trees and greenery to create more shade, by retrofitting homes and apartment buildings with rooftop gardens or reflective roofing, and by using solar panels. This could all be done with the<a href=\"https://www.npr.org/2021/05/11/993976948/reaching-back-to-the-new-deal-biden-proposes-a-civilian-climate-corps\"> Civilian Climate Corps</a> that has been proposed by the Biden administration</p><p>We could also deal with future heatwaves like we do the pandemic. Declare a public health emergency and ask people to stay home when they can, close businesses and mandate heat leave pay, and offer transportation to cooling centers. For those still working essential jobs, provide heat hazard pay and labor protections.</p><p>Currently, California is one of the<a href=\"https://civileats.com/2021/06/14/as-the-climate-emergency-grows-farmworkers-lack-protection-from-deadly-heat/\"> few states</a> that have labor protections for heat built into law - the others being Minnesota and Washington. Despite attempts in Congress over the past few years to do so, there are no federal labor standards that address heat. There is currently a<a href=\"https://edlabor.house.gov/media/press-releases/house-senate-leaders-introduce-heat-stress-legislation-to-protect-farm-workers\"> bill</a> in Congress sponsored by Reps Judy Chu, Bobby Scott, Raúl M. Grijalva, and Alma Adams that would direct OSHA to issue a federal standard on heat.</p><p>None of this is a substitute for real climate action of course; but while we need to take necessary and immediate action to drop emissions, we also need to deal with the present public health consequences of our failure to take action earlier.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on (Abdullah Shihipar)","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i.insider.com/60fad76ffc2536001820115d?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn64@2020_4/2021/07/25/10-54-24-298_18e9ba013577d06a.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn39@2020_3/2021/07/25/10-54-25-234_cc3ef43fc8ff5801.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=N-bk6rZtEk4:J2b0QkyE1wA:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn80@2020_5/2021/07/25/10-54-25-977_56f217df6b55be27.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=N-bk6rZtEk4:J2b0QkyE1wA:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn3@2020_4/2021/07/25/10-54-23-291_5ab3aea705d9f0c1.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn95@2020_6/2021/07/25/10-54-23-641_740defb435ea442b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=N-bk6rZtEk4:J2b0QkyE1wA:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn71@2020_4/2021/07/25/10-54-22-245_4a3fa8c9644d31f7.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/N-bk6rZtEk4":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn11@2020_5/2021/07/25/10-54-22-616_7f27959c1e1a302c.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1627210446782},{"createdTime":"2021-07-25 18:54:06","updatedTime":"2021-07-25 18:54:06","title":"Leisure travel has made a startling comeback, but business travel's recovery could still be years away","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/leisure-travel-coming-back-experts-business-travel-recovery-years-out-2021-7","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/60f97f2ffc253600181fcbea?format=jpeg\" alt=\"A TSA officer wears a mask at Logan International Airport in Boston in March 2020.\" height=\"3214\" width=\"4286\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>A TSA officer wears a mask at Logan International Airport in Boston in March 2020.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Brian Snyder/Reuters</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Leisure travel is making a strong comeback, but corporate travel is still lagging behind.</li>\n<li>Combination business and leisure travel is &#34;cushioning the blow,&#34; but won&#39;t save business travel hotels.</li>\n<li>Patrick Scholes, an analyst at Truist Securities, believes business travel won&#39;t fully recover until 2023.</li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>It seems like everyone in the US is rushing to <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/hawaii-travel-restrictions-ease-as-locals-tourism-covid-vaccine-2021-7\">book their summer &#34;revenge vacation.&#34;</a> But there&#39;s one segment of travel that&#39;s lagging behind: <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/singapore-bring-business-travelers-back-with-a-bubble-hotel-2021-1\">business travel</a>.</p><p>Most of us might be laser-focused on leisure vacations right now, but the revival of business travel is crucial for hospitality and travel companies. Before COVID-19, <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/experts-predict-when-airline-business-class-travel-will-return-2021-7\">business travelers made up</a> 12% to 15% of trips on larger airlines but generated about 45% of airlines&#39; revenue.</p><p>And while leisure demand has passed complete recovery, business travel is still lagging behind by 60%, Scott Kirby, United Airlines CEO&#39;s, told John Dickerson on CBS&#39; &#34;<a href=\"https://www.cbsnews.com/news/united-airlines-ceo-scott-kirby-on-face-the-nation-july-11-2021/\">Face the Nation</a>.&#34; Similarly Southwest Airlines&#39; &#34;business travel component&#34; was down 69%  in June, Gary Kelly, Southwest Airlines chairman and CEO, told <a href=\"https://www.bloomberg.com/news/videos/2021-07-22/southwest-ceo-kelly-on-second-quarter-business-travel-video\">Bloomberg</a>. Kelly predicts this segment will continue to be down 50% by September, and will then improve afterwards.</p><p>In 2022 and 2023, Southwest plans to bring back more flights catered to business customers.</p><p>The consensus is that business travel will accelerate in September after Labor Day, Patrick Scholes, an analyst at Truist Securities, told Insider. And 63% of companies plan to bring back business travel within the next one to three months, according to a <a href=\"https://www.gbta.org/Portals/0/Coronavirus_Recovery_Poll_Key_Highlights_07-19-21.pdf?ver=2021-07-16-151909-503\">Global Business Travel Association</a> survey of over 600 companies.</p><p><em><strong>Read more: </strong><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/experts-predict-when-airline-business-class-travel-will-return-2021-7\">Lost business class travel is pummeling big airlines. Industry leaders predict when it might return - and how it could change.</a></em></p><p>&#34;I don&#39;t think anything will be normal on the other side of this, but we expect that business demand is really going to pick up in September as most of these schools are back in,&#34; Kirby said.</p><p>Those who are &#34;bullish&#34; are holding out for a September business travel increase that mimics the recent skyrocket of leisure travel, Scholes said. But he believes there will be a &#34;modest acceleration&#34; after the federal holiday, and that the segment will still be down compared to 2019. </p><p>&#34;Some of the more bullish folks in the greater industry think it&#39;ll be a full recovery by the end of next year,&#34; Scholes said. &#34;Some of the more conservative, perhaps not until 2024.&#34;</p><p>Both Scholes and Kirby fall somewhere in between: They believe business travel will be back by the end of 2023.</p><h2>The impact on hotels</h2><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/60587fdb1fcb5700186eea2e?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Airport pandemic covid travel Illinois\" height=\"1998\" width=\"2664\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Travelers arrive for flights at O&#39;Hare international Airport on March 16, 2021 in Chicago, Illinois.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Scott Olson/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>It&#39;s not just airlines: The slow and steady increase in business travel is also impacting hotels.</p><p>From June 21 to June 24 (a Monday through Thursday when business travel typically happens) the revenue per available room for generic business travel hotels was down about 45% compared to the same time in 2019. Meanwhile, mid-week stays at economy hotels - like Super 8, which primarily caters to leisure travelers - were up almost 10%.</p><p>Luckily, the <a href=\"https://www.usatoday.com/story/travel/hotels/2021/05/11/business-and-leisure-travel-beginning-blend-marriott-ceo-says/5035270001/\">increasingly blurred line between business and leisure travel</a> has &#34;helped cushion the blow,&#34; Scholes said. But even this won&#39;t &#34;ever fully account for that Wednesday night business travel.&#34;</p><p>As a result, &#34;the road to a full recovery for America&#39;s hotels is long and uneven,&#34; Chip Rogers, president and CEO of American Hotel and Lodging Association (AHLA) said in a <a href=\"https://www.ahla.com/press-release/reports-nearly-500000-hotel-jobs-wont-return-years-end-room-revenue-will-be-down-44\">press release</a>. Like other experts, AHLA predicts business travel won&#39;t fully recover until 2023 or 2024, citing that many conferences, conventions, and in-person events have already been pushed back until 2022.</p><p>Business travel recovery depends on factors like persistent COVID-19 concerns, the rise in Zoom, and the newfound work from home comfort that has replaced the time and stress of commuting. However, Scholes doesn&#39;t believe these factors will permanently slice business travel in half, which is<a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/bill-gates-business-travel-office-work-predictions-post-pandemic-2020-11\"> what Bill Gates predicted in 2020</a>.</p><p>&#34;In my view, that is way off,&#34; Scholes said, predicting that about 5% of business travel customers will be lost in the end.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a
